Title ^uCLvzK8i

Key-words ^9iJSs8cK

Abstract ^Zqj01vtn

Intro ^OTzA9ejB

1. Contextual Landscape
2. Problem Statement
3. Hypothesis / Research Question(s)
4. Objectives and Significance
5. Preview of Structure ^KRX8oczK

Methodology ^tT1B3W0U

1. Study/Experiment Design

2. Participants / Sample / Dataset:
- Size
- Source
- Inclusion/exclusion criteria

3. Materials and Apparatus
- Instruments (e.g., EEG device, survey software, cell cultures)
- Calibration procedures

4. Procedure
- Step-by-step operations
- Timing, duration, stimuli, tasks

5. Data Collection Protocol:
- What was captured, how, when, by whom

6. Analytical Methods:
- Statistical models, qualitative coding frameworks, computational tools
- Software and libraries
- Assumptions and thresholds (e.g., p < 0.05)
 ^sn5OuQJB

Results ^6i1Ikipn

1. Descriptive Statistics: Means, medians, distributions.
2. Inferential Statistics: Tests, regressions, significance levels.
3. Figures & Tables: Visual renderings of the data matrix.
4. Subsectioning: Results may be structured by research question or data type. ^eBlATNRT

Discussion ^J5zZUx7B

1. Interpretation of Findings: What do the results mean in light of the hypotheses?
2. Comparative Literature Analysis: Where do these findings sit in the scholarly constellation?
3. Implications: Theoretical shifts, practical applications, paradigm disruptions.
4. Limitations: Sources of error, generalizability constraints, methodological caveats.
5. Future Directions: What rituals remain to be performed?

 ^N7QkMlFV

Conclusion ^ZxCw2FG9

Restate the contribution. Affirm the insight. Open the next door. ^lSxNZQ1O

Topic 0 – "Human Brain & Cognitive Correlation"
Words like dimension, similarity, correlation, regression,
human brain, context, sci

This topic seems focused on cognitive science, brain
correlation models, and thought experiment design ^bzVuvytW

Topic 3 well isolated → reinforces that
speech/hearing/spectrogram is semantically distant from the
other topics

Topics 0 and 1 are close → may indicate that cognitive science
and experimental semantics share vocabulary

Topics 2, 4, 5 have slight overlap → should have common
jargon (embedding, classification, text alignment) ^xvt4A1NQ

Topic 1 – "Semantic Encoding"
semantic, vectors, experiment, unifb, passages

Very cohesive: talks about semantic representations in
controlled experiments, possibly related to BERT,
embeddings, semantic vectors, etc. ^3VFnoHh9

Topic 2 – "Language Modeling & EEG/fMRI fusion"
fmri, meg, eeg, word, bert, sentiment, finetuned

A very strong topic about language modeling with
neural data, mixing signals (eeg/fMRI) with modern
embeddings ^nEwld5jE

Topic 3 – "Auditory Spectrograms & Modulation"
spectrogram, modulation, auditory, ecog, decoded

Most likely related to acoustic representations, voice and
auditory neural decoding ^L8txnw4r

Topic 4 – "EEG Classification & Embeddings"
eeg, class, embeddings, contextual, classification, svm

Points to classical EEG classification approaches with
embeddings and context vectors ^WYUBPj0x

Topic 5 – "Text + Brain Semantic Alignment"
semantic, clip, eeg, diffusion, vol, text, image

A combination of text-to-brain alignment, multimodality,
and models like CLIP/BERT to link language and brain
signals ^kGyr4gkE

BIDS ^fdWNJnb1

BIDS (Brain Imaging Data Structure)

Standard folder structure and metadata
(JSON/TSV) for organizing and sharing
neuroimaging data. ^XXbXhBLt

University of West Bohemia in Pilsen
(Czech Republic) ^koq8lgeg

These were pioneering efforts toward
standardization and have influenced
BIDS EEG and other frameworks. ^OycmnoZM

Ontario Brain Institute (Canada) ^G2hkXizx

A popular data science competition
platform (owned by Google, launched
2010). Not specific to EEG, but has
hosted EEG datasets for machine
learning challenges (e.g., seizure
prediction contests, EEG emotion
recognition) ^eTx7AUz3

It is one of the largest archives for brain
imaging data, including EEG, iEEG, MEG,
MRI, etc. ^3n5ww6DE

Why do people use
umbrellas today? ^vy0PWC4u

Main Components of an Umbrella: 

Shaft:  The central part that supports the entire umbrella. It is
the backbone of the structure, which connects the handle to the
dome. It can be made of steel, aluminum, ﬁberglass or plastic.

Handle: The part by which we hold the umbrella. It can be
straight or curved, and is usually made of wood, plastic, rubber
or metal. Some have ergonomic coatings for greater comfort.

Ribs:  These are the thin rods that support the umbrella's
canopy and form the dome when it is open. There are two main
types: Main ribs:  The main structure that gives shape to the
umbrella. Secondary ribs (or stretchers): Connect the main ribs
to the shaft and help to open and close the umbrella. 

Top Cap or Ferrule:  The piece at the top of the umbrella, used to
protect the end of the shaft and may also have an aesthetic
purpose. 

Canopy:  The fabric that forms the dome of the umbrella,
protecting it from rain or sun. Tips:  The ends of the rods that
help to secure the canopy fabric. ^Ebyu8aea

Instruction Manual: Using the Umbrella Opening Hold the
handle ﬁrmly with your dominant hand. Locate the
automatic opening button (if available) or, on manual
umbrellas, slide the sliding ring to the upper position.
Make sure the canopy is fully open before proceeding.
Positioning Hold the umbrella directly above your head,
with the shaft perpendicular to the ground. Adjust the
angle to protect your body from rain or sun, avoiding
exposure to the front or back. Walking Move at a steady
speed, ensuring that the umbrella maintains balance.
Avoid close contact with other people to prevent
accidents. Closing When you are ﬁnished using the
umbrella, press the closing button (if automatic) or retract
the sliding ring to retract the canopy. Make sure the
umbrella is dry before storing it. Storing Store the
umbrella in a dry environment to prevent corrosion. If it is
humid, leave it open until it is completely dry. ^GAeNNeD1

This umbrella is manual, with a plastic
handle, aluminum shaft and nylon canopy.
This cover has a yellowish beige background
and drawings of plants and birds. ^UsHbvOkk

Umbrella User's Guide To use an umbrella, you must
ﬁrst pay attention to the sky. It may act like it doesn't
understand, a blue or gray accomplice, and you may
think that the rain isn't coming. But when you smell the
damp smell in the wind, you must act: hold the handle
ﬁrmly, remembering that the umbrella, by its nature, is
temperamental. Open it without much fright, because it
is startled by the suddenness, and gently push the
button. As soon as the dome opens above you, feel
yourself ﬂoating slightly, as if you were hovering in a
small world of plastic and sticks – and, oh, how
enchanting it will be. Remember not to walk too fast, nor
too slow, because the umbrella, being proud, does not
follow in haste and will resent dragging it along the
sidewalk. Finally, when you see the ﬁrst ray of sunlight,
lower it with a silent bow, because it has fulﬁlled its
purpose and demands its well- deserved rest. ^AoS9cxAe

2000 BCE – Origins in Ancient Egypt and China: In Ancient Egypt, around 2000
BCE, umbrella- like structures were created to protect royalty from the sun, not
the rain. These umbrellas were status symbols and were made from palm
leaves and feathers. In China, records show umbrellas made of waterproof
oiled paper. They were the ﬁrst to apply a layer of wax or oil to the paper to
repel water, making them the ﬁrst functional umbrella for rain. ^W6SBa3e8

5th Century BC – Popularity in Ancient Greece and
Rome: In Ancient Greece, the umbrella was used by
women to protect themselves from the sun and
indicated elegance. In Rome, it became a status
symbol among elite women. In Rome, men began to
use umbrellas as a sign of status and style, but they
were still considered feminine items. ^dBGGz00O

16th Century – Popularization in Europe: In
Europe, the umbrella began to appear in Italy
and spread to other countries, such as France
and England. However, it was still seen mainly
as a form of protection against the sun, known
as a “parasol”. It was made of silk and oiled
paper, and the frame was made of wood,
bamboo or ivory. ^54iRhsg1

18th Century – Introduction of the Rain
Umbrella in England: In 1750, Jonas
Hanway, an Englishman, popularized the
use of the umbrella in England for
protection from the rain. He was initially
ridiculed for carrying an umbrella, but over
time the idea caught on. Manufacturers
began experimenting with waterproof
fabrics and frame materials such as
whalebone steel, making the umbrella
more durable and practical. ^m7Gla2DB

1830 – First Umbrella Factory in London: The
ﬁrst umbrella factory was established by James
Smith & Sons in London, which remains in
operation to this day. They perfected the design
and made the umbrella more aﬀordable for the
general public. ^hSA6ss7V

19th Century – Innovations
in Materials and
Mechanisms: Steel replaced
bamboo in the shafts,
providing greater strength
and durability. Fabric also
evolved into water-
resistant synthetic
materials. Mass production
made the umbrella
aﬀordable, and it became a
staple in Western countries. ^BIQtprpl

1928 – Introduction of the Folding Umbrella:
In 1928, Hans Haupt, a German engineer,
invented the ﬁrst folding umbrella. This
invention made the umbrella portable, making
it easier to transport and store. 1969 – The
Automatic Umbrella: In 1969, Samuel Fox
invented the automatic- opening umbrella.
This type of umbrella allowed the canopy to
be opened at the touch of a button, which
made it very popular. ^wwJLqhX7

2000s – Modern Materials and Compact Design:
In the 2000s, the use of glass ﬁbers and high-
strength polymers improved the ﬂexibility and
durability of umbrellas, especially in high wind
conditions. Compact, foldable designs continued
to evolve, with ultra- lightweight, durable
umbrellas becoming the norm. ^xga8zpsH

Smart Umbrellas – Recent Innovations: In
recent years, smart umbrellas have emerged
that incorporate rain sensors and weather
alerts, connecting to mobile devices and
oﬀering real- time weather forecasts. ^8q2AYsMH

women ^2i08phX2

We now live in a future where computation is no longer
made of silicon, but of living matter. In this world, people
buy biocomputer seeds. Each seed carries a unique code.
When planted, watered, and cared for like a plant, it
grows into a hybrid organism: a living computer, sensitive
to its surroundings. It grows like a plant but thinks like a
machine — nourished by light, warmth, and care. This
new form of AI doesn’t live in the cloud; it breathes,
pulses, and connects organically. Within this scenario,
your object will be transformed into an interface to
imagine alongside a living intelligence. ^7nJW1d8Q
